Or has Facebook built a contraption that neither Facebook nor anyone else can really "fix"?
Easy to see how this task, done at scale, with a lot of better-compensated human input, would turn Facebook from a tech unicorn into closer to a traditional (and less profitable) media company. But is it even possible to do well?
But few people seem satisfied with the job they are doing. Different critics have different opinions about how adaptable FB should be to local rules &amp; customs. And as Casey and other reporters have shown it is a Sisyphean task overall.
FB in the past faced a lot of criticism for wanting to have it both ways as a platform -- all the content and little or no responsibility for it. Now, responding to public pressure, they are moderating more content.

4) I think what any general interest outlet wants to be doing is ensuring that a reader following its coverage can be reasonably well-informed about a range of things -- what's happening that day, how the candidates contrast, what they've proposed doing in office.
3) News Outlet X ran three "horse race" stories for every one "policy" story is not a particularly useful metric for evaluating coverage. The term "horse race," as it is applied in some critiques, is both simplistic and opaque.
2) Voters do not always make decisions based on policy. Maybe that's bad. It's also a reality of human politics since we lived in caves (or had voters, or democracies).
There's a couple of things I hope critics, professional and otherwise, will also keep in mind:	1) Candidates do not communicate largely or even chiefly in formal policy proposals. They tell stories --about themselves, about the country.
Appreciate all the responses and counterpoints people have raised here. It‚Äôs worth all of us thinking about.

Not endorsing this argument, simply laying out what I think is an interesting case. Think of it as buying back your government from special interests.
If you paid Members and staff like other well-educated professionals, you might also be able to slow down the revolving door. As it is, moving out to K Street is now basically an assumed part of the congressional lifecycle.

But ICO also debunks a few things. Notwithstanding a variety of contradictory statements from CA officials on the subject, the report states that Cambridge never did any actual work for https://t.co/L2w3sV1sJk.
It also seems that Cambridge made at least some attempt to delete Facebook data in its possession right after the ICO announced its investigation.	Nevertheless, ICO found that Cambridge in 2018 retained (at least) proprietary models based on the improperly obtained Facebook data.
Worse: When the warrant was served, ICO investigators found "a number of disconnected and physically damaged servers." The suggestion is that Cambridge employees may have tried to destroy evidence.
Cambridge itself was equally sloppy with the Facebook data it obtained. When the ICO executed a search warrant, it found passwords written on Post-it notes stuck to walls. They used personal email accounts to transfer data. They didn't encrypt transfers.

Here's the super-detailed version of the IA's proposal. 	https://t.co/UaSeR1JsiL	Notably, the @InternetAssn's former regulatory affairs chief is now Trump's White House senior advisor on tech.
The proposal suggests that the FTC take the lead on enforcing new rules, but does not specify any particular new powers or authority for the FTC--a fairly small agency whose statutory authority rests heavily on questions of, more or less, false advertising.
The industry wants any national law to pre-empt both existing state privacy and data security laws AND data breach notification laws. Significantly, many states have already achieved tough rules in these areas.
Fundamentally, and not surprisingly, the industry wants a "risk-based" approach, meaning companies are punished for misusing your information only when you can prove "tangible harm." I.e., consumer privacy would not be an inherent right.
Here it is: The @InternetAssn, tech's leading (and Google + Facebook-dominated) trade group, releases principles for a national privacy proposal. https://t.co/5QFxU23ukK Some thoughts:

@antoniogm Yes--exactly my point.
@antoniogm So was Facebook lying when they put out the statement criticizing the California privacy bill, which didn't have the private right of action?	Google and Facebook's trade associations also went hard against the bill.	And even now they are lobbying to weaken it in Sacto.
Sam Patten, a Manafort associate, pleads guilty for failing to register as a foreign agent: https://t.co/Iw8Lgls0LH	N.B. Patten also had a relationship with Cambridge Analytica from 2014 until earlier this year.
Is it all an elaborate con? Maybe. @jason_kint and others have argued that GDPR is only "good" for these companies if they flout the letter of law or lobby regulators to adopt their preferred interpretation of it. True! That's always a danger in government regulation.
So if this law is so good for them, why did they dislike it? Facebook's explanation is that they just didn't like the private right of action. But lots of folks think it goes deeper. Any move toward basic privacy regulations is dangerous to a surveillance-based business model.
Nevertheless, Facebook and Google both fought pretty aggressively against both the original California initiative and the final law. I think it's always useful to look at what companies do as much as what they say.
As I wrote in the story: Mactaggart never set out to "hurt" Google and Facebook. He and his team made a deliberate decision to focus on third-party data-selling/swapping. He thought he could get rid of the creepiest stuff that way.
I appreciate @antoniogm's link to my @NYTmag piece on California's new privacy data regulation, but I think his analysis -- like a number of "Facebook always wins you suckers!" stories -- misses the mark. Here's Antonio's piece:  https://t.co/ttJqrONTG6

The Cambridge Analytica scandal scrambled the old playbook, in part because it indelibly associated Silicon Valley's lucrative data mining with -- fairly or not, Trump's victory. In California, Mactaggart's ballot initiative was suddenly very popular.
The playbook: Argue that privacy rules are unnecessary because "no one cares." Or too technically difficult to implement. Or will make it hard to catch criminals. Or kill jobs. It worked--until California, and until Cambridge Analytica.
He first tried to negotiate w/Facebook &amp; Google. Mactaggart sat with both companies last year to discuss a compromise. A couple months later, they secretly started funding a campaign to crush him. Dems in California led the charge, using what had become a familiar playbook. https://t.co/TkjFTrPjQf
Mactaggart had money, and passion, but no fixed idea about how to tackle privacy. He hooked up with a few privacy vets, including @JustinBrookman, @hoofnagle, and @ashk4n, who came on as a consultant. The lessons of Washington, Illinois, and other battles shaped their plan.
Around the same time, in California, a Bay Area real estate developer named Alastair Mactaggart was getting interested in privacy issues, after dinner with a Google engineer who told him how scared people would be if they knew what Google knew about them. https://t.co/VL4ks8QDYK
